The text beneath included nuggets of wisdom such as &ldquo;stay quiet&rdquo; and &ldquo;take a laxative&rdquo;. Oh, and to apply their ointment liberally, of course.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe 1918 flu pandemic was \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20181016-the-flu-that-transformed-the-20th-century\"\u003Ethe most lethal in recorded history\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, infecting up to 500 million people (a quarter of the world&rsquo;s population at the time) and killing tens of millions worldwide.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut with crisis comes opportunity, and the &ndash; sometimes literal &ndash; snake oil salesmen were out in force. Vick&rsquo;s VapoRub had stiff competition from a panoply of crackpot remedies, including \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.eiu.edu\u002Fhistoria\u002F2010Shidler.pdf\"\u003EMiller&rsquo;s Antiseptic Snake Oil\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, Dr Bell&rsquo;s Pine Tar Honey, Schenck&rsquo;s Mandrake Pills, Dr Jones&rsquo;s Liniment, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.laphamsquarterly.org\u002Froundtable\u002Feat-more-onions\"\u003EHill&rsquo;s Cascara Quinine Bromide\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and A. Wulfing &amp; Co&rsquo;s famous mint lozenges. Their adverts made regular appearances in the newspapers, where they starred alongside increasingly alarming headlines.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFast-forward to 2020, and not much has changed. Though the Covid-19 pandemic is separated from the Spanish flu by over a century of scientific discoveries, there are still plenty of questionable medicinal concoctions and folk remedies floating around. This time, the theme is &ldquo;boosting&rdquo; the immune system.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOf the rumours currently circulating on social media, one of the more bizarre is the idea that you can raise your white blood cell count by masturbating more. And as always, nutritional advice abounds. This time, we&rsquo;re being encouraged to seek out foods rich in antioxidants and vitamin C (back in 1918, the public were told to eat more onions), while pseudoscientists are peddling trendy products such as \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.nutraingredients.com\u002FArticle\u002F2020\u002F03\u002F23\u002FMicrobiome-maintenance-Good-gut-health-could-tackle-COVID-19\"\u003Ekombucha\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.biogaia.com\u002Fother-news\u002Fcovid-19-outbreak-and-probiotics-facts\u002F\"\u003Eprobiotics\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYou might also like:\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200330-coronavirus-are-children-immune-to-covid-19\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EWhy children are not immune to Covid-19\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cbr \u002F\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200327-can-you-kill-coronavirus-with-uv-light\" target=\"_blank\"\u003ECan you kill coronavirus with UV light? \u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200406-why-smart-people-believe-coronavirus-myths\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EWhy smart people believe coronavirus myths\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAccording to one source, cayenne pepper and green tea can provide better protection against Covid-19\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.mirror.co.uk\u002Flifestyle\u002Fhealth\u002F7-ways-improve-your-immune-21580462\"\u003E than face masks\u003C\u002Fa\u003E &ndash; a bold and highly dubious claim, considering that some face masks reduce your risk of contracting respiratory viruses by a factor of five. (\u003Cem\u003ERead more about \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200406-are-there-benefits-to-eating-turmeric-and-other-spices\"\u003Ewhat evidence exists for the idea that spices can affect your health\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and how \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19\"\u003Ehot drinks will not protect you from Covid-19\u003C\u002Fa\u003E).\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThere&rsquo;s no such thing as boosted immunity\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EUnfortunately, the idea that pills, trendy superfoods or wellness habits can provide a shortcut to a healthy immune system is a myth. In fact, the concept of &ldquo;boosting&rdquo; your immune system doesn&rsquo;t hold any scientific meaning whatsoever.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;There are three different components to immunity,&rdquo; says Akiko Iwasaki, an immunologist at Yale University. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s things like skin, the airways and the mucus membranes that are there to begin with, and they provide a barrier to infection. But once the virus gets past these defences, then you have to induce the &lsquo;innate&rsquo; immune response.&rdquo; This consists of chemicals and cells which can rapidly raise the alert and begin fighting off any intruder.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"infographic","imageAlignment":"centre","imageOrientation":"landscape","pullQuoteImageAlignment":"centre","videoImageAlign":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-0"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p0890nv4"],"imageAlignment":"centre","imageAltText":"An Italian advert for a remedy during the 1918 flu pandemic (Credit: Getty Images)","imageCaption":"The 1918 flu pandemic was an opportunity for snake oil salesmen to market their useless - and sometimes harmful - products (Credit: Getty Images)","imageOrientation":"landscape","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-1"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;When that is not enough, then we kick in the adaptive immune system,&rdquo; she says. This involves cells and proteins &ndash; antibodies &ndash; which take a few days or weeks to emerge. Importantly, the adaptive immune system can only target particular pathogens. &ldquo;So, for example, a T-cell specific to Covid-19 will not respond to influenza or bacterial pathogens.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMost infections will trigger adaptive immunity eventually. But there's another way to get it going, and that's vaccination: exposing the body to live or dead microbes, or parts of them, can help the body to identify the real deal when it comes along.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe concept of &ldquo;boosting&rdquo; a person&rsquo;s immune system would, presumably, involve making these responses more active, or stronger.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn actuality, you wouldn&rsquo;t want to do this.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETake the symptoms of a cold &ndash; body aches, a fever, brain fog, copious amounts of snot and phlegm. Most of these problems aren&rsquo;t actually caused by the virus itself. Instead, they&rsquo;re triggered by your own body, on purpose: they&rsquo;re part of the innate immune response.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"Many “immunity-boosting” products claim to reduce inflammation","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EIn this case, the mucus helps to flush out the pathogen, the fever helps to make your body an uncomfortably hot environment in which it&rsquo;s harder for it to replicate, and the aches and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.sciencemag.org\u002Fnews\u002F2016\u002F04\u002Fhere-s-why-you-feel-so-crummy-when-you-re-sick\"\u003Egeneral malaise\u003C\u002Fa\u003E are by-products of the inflammatory chemicals that course through your veins, telling immune cells what to do and where to go. (These symptoms also help signal to your brain that it&rsquo;s time to slow down and let your body recover).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe mucus and chemical signals are part of inflammation, which is \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpmc\u002Farticles\u002FPMC3282411\u002F\"\u003Ethe bedrock of a healthy immune response\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. But the process is exhausting, so you wouldn&rsquo;t want to have it turned up to 11 all the time. And most viruses, including Covid-19, will trigger it anyway. If kombucha, green tea or any of the various &ldquo;immune-boosting&rdquo; concoctions on the market really had any impact, they wouldn&rsquo;t give you a healthful glow: they&rsquo;d give you a runny nose.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIronically, many &ldquo;immunity-boosting&rdquo; products claim to reduce inflammation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-4"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p0890mvk"],"imageAlignment":"centre","imageAltText":"Empty vitamin shelves in a supermarket (Credit: Reuters)","imageCaption":"There is no evidence that vitamin supplements will protect you from infections, unless you are deficient (Credit: Reuters)","imageOrientation":"landscape","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-5"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EMaking the other aspect of immunity &ndash; the adaptive immune system &ndash; generally more active could also be extremely unpleasant. For example, allergies occur when overzealous immune cells learn to treat innocuous foreign bodies, such as pollen, as though they are harmful. Each time they find the offending substance, they switch on the innate immune response too &ndash; cue lots of sneezing, itchy eyes and general fatigue. Again, this is probably not what the people championing these remedies have in mind.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut let&rsquo;s give those saying you can &ldquo;boost&rdquo; your immune system the benefit of the doubt and assume they mean that certain products can improve the immune response in a useful way &ndash; rather than literally &ldquo;boost&rdquo; it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;The problem is that many of these claims have no grounding in evidence,&rdquo; Iwasaki says. So what are they based on &ndash; and is there anything that can help?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIf you&rsquo;re healthy, forget supplements &ndash; except vitamin D\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMany multivitamins claim to provide &ldquo;immune support&rdquo; or to help &ldquo;maintain healthy immune function&rdquo;. But as BBC Future reported in 2016, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20161208-why-vitamin-supplements-could-kill-you\"\u003Evitamin supplements generally don&rsquo;t work\u003C\u002Fa\u003E in already healthy people &ndash; and some may even be harmful.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETake vitamin C. The health effects of this antioxidant have been steeped in mythology ever since the two-time Nobel Prize winner Linus Pauling became obsessed with its ability to fight the common cold. After studying the vitamin for years, eventually he started taking 18,000 mg per day &ndash; around 300 times the current recommended daily amount.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-6"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"Vitamin supplements aren’t beneficial to your immune system unless you are deficient","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-7"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EHowever, there is little evidence to support vitamin C&rsquo;s mighty reputation for helping us to fight off colds and other respiratory infections. A \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.cochrane.org\u002FCD000980\u002FARI_vitamin-c-for-preventing-and-treating-the-common-cold\"\u003E2013 review\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by Cochrane &ndash; an organisation renowned for its unbiased research &ndash; found that in adults &ldquo;trials of high doses of vitamin C administered therapeutically, starting after the onset of symptoms, showed no consistent effect on the duration or severity of common cold symptoms&rdquo;.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn fact, many experts consider the vitamin C market \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.vox.com\u002F2015\u002F1\u002F15\u002F7547741\u002Fvitamin-c-myth-pauling\"\u003Eto be a bit of a racket\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, as most people in the developed world get enough from their diets already. Though scurvy is thought to have killed two million sailors and pirates between the 15th and 18th Centuries, the numbers now are far lower. For example, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftheconversation.com\u002Fscurvy-is-it-really-making-a-comeback-in-the-uk-124841\"\u003Ejust 128 people in England\u003C\u002Fa\u003E were hospitalised with the disease between 2016 and 2017. On the other hand, high doses of this vitamin can \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.healthline.com\u002Fnutrition\u002Fside-effects-of-too-much-vitamin-c\"\u003Elead to kidney stones\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Vitamin supplements aren&rsquo;t beneficial to your immune system unless you are deficient,&rdquo; says Iwasaki.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-8"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p0890mhq"],"imageAlignment":"centre","imageAltText":"An assortment of vegetables in a wooden crate (Credit: Getty Images)","imageCaption":"Brightly coloured fruits and vegetables tend to contain the most antioxidants, because the compounds are often pigmented (Credit: Getty Images)","imageOrientation":"landscape","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-9"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EIn the developed world, most people get enough vitamins from their diets (unless they are restricted &ndash; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200127-how-a-vegan-diet-could-affect-your-intelligence\"\u003Evegans, for example, are more likely to have certain deficiencies\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). However, there is one exception &ndash; vitamin D. Iwasaki explains that taking this supplement wouldn&rsquo;t be a bad idea.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESeveral studies have linked low vitamin D levels to a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F19237723\"\u003Ehigher risk of respiratory infections\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and more severe symptoms when they develop. They&rsquo;ve also been \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.sciencedaily.com\u002Freleases\u002F2019\u002F04\u002F190417111440.htm\"\u003Eimplicated in the development of autoimmune diseases\u003C\u002Fa\u003E such as multiple sclerosis.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn fact, many immune cells can actively recognise vitamin D, and it&rsquo;s \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpmc\u002Farticles\u002FPMC3166406\u002F\"\u003Ethought to play an important role in both the innate and acquired immune response\u003C\u002Fa\u003E &ndash; though exactly how remains a mystery.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut crucially &ndash; and unusually &ndash; vitamin D deficiencies are endemic in many countries, even wealthy ones. As of 2012, it was estimated that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F22629085\"\u003Eabout a billion people worldwide\u003C\u002Fa\u003E weren&rsquo;t getting enough. And with more and more people urged to stay indoors, it&rsquo;s easy to see how even less sunlight exposure could lead to more deficiencies. \u003Cem\u003E(Read more about \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20181010-do-vitamin-d-supplements-work\"\u003Ewho needs to take vitamin D and why\u003C\u002Fa\u003E).\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENo, masturbation won&rsquo;t help either \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHistorically, this form of sexual activity was held in deep suspicion by Western medicine. After an 18th Century doctor claimed that the loss of one ounce of semen (28 millilitres) had the same effect on the body as losing 40 ounces (1.18 litres) of blood, masturbation was blamed for all kinds of health problems for hundreds of years, from blindness to neurosis.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENow the tables have turned, and recent research has shown that it can come with some surprising health benefits. In men, for example, it&rsquo;s thought to help keep sperm healthy and may \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.europeanurology.com\u002Farticle\u002FS0302-2838%2816%2900377-8\u002Ffulltext\"\u003Ereduce a person&rsquo;s risk of developing prostate cancer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-10"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"The question of whether antioxidants can help is slightly more complicated","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-11"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EAlas, any claims that masturbation can improve your immunity or protect you from Covid-19 are overblown. It&rsquo;s true that one study found that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F15316239\"\u003Emen had higher white blood cell counts\u003C\u002Fa\u003E when they were sexually aroused, and during orgasm. However, there is no evidence that this translates into protection from infections.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere is one way that the practice might protect you &ndash; by keeping away from other people. On Twitter, the&nbsp;New York City Department of Health and Mental Hygiene recently reminded their followers that, in the age of Covid-19, &ldquo;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002FnycHealthy\u002Fstatus\u002F1242462479134982145?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1242462479134982145&amp;ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Ftwitter.com%2FnycHealthy%2Fstatus%2F1242462479134982145\"\u003Eyou are your safest sex partner\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&rdquo;.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EThere&rsquo;s no need to stock up on antioxidant pills\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe question of whether antioxidants can help is slightly more complicated.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs part of the inflammatory response, white blood cells release toxic oxygen compounds. These are something of a double-edged sword. On the one hand, they can kill bacteria and viruses and stop them from being able to make more copies of themselves. On the other, they can damage healthy cells, leading to cancer and ageing &ndash; and wearing out the immune system.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETo stop this from happening, the body relies on antioxidants. These help to control those unruly oxygen compounds and keep our cells safe.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAnd we get some of our reserves of these compounds from our diets. Brightly coloured fruits, vegetables and spices tend to contain the most, because antioxidants are often pigmented: they give carrots, blueberries, aubergines, red kale, turmeric, and strawberries their hues.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-12"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p0890pks"],"imageAlignment":"centre","imageAltText":"A glass of kombucha, a type of fermented tea (Credit: Getty Images)","imageCaption":"Wellness experts like to promote kombucha as more than just a drink - but there's no evidence that it can treat or prevent any illnesses, including Covid-19 (Credit: Getty Images)","imageOrientation":"landscape","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-13"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EThere&rsquo;s currently a trial in the works to test if giving \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fclinicaltrials.gov\u002Fct2\u002Fshow\u002FNCT04323228\"\u003Epeople with Covid-19 antioxidant supplements\u003C\u002Fa\u003E might help their recovery.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHowever, the trial is just one of hundreds looking into potential treatments for Covid-19. And despite decades of research, not a single placebo-controlled, peer-reviewed study on humans has ever shown that high doses of antioxidants can &ldquo;boost&rdquo; the immune system, or treat or prevent viral infections in humans.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EProbiotics may help&hellip; or they may not\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIf you believe the wellness experts and homeopaths, kombucha is much more than a sweet, fizzy drink made from fermented tea. The internet is teeming with outrageous claims about the product, including that it can \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.salon.com\u002F2018\u002F05\u002F20\u002Fit-took-food-poisoning-losing-7500-and-an-intervention-to-make-me-realize-my-kombucha-problem\u002F\"\u003Etreat cancer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and even \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ffood52.com\u002Fblog\u002F20366-queer-history-of-kombucha\"\u003EAids\u003C\u002Fa\u003E (it can&rsquo;t).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENow some websites are suggesting that it can \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.nutraingredients.com\u002FArticle\u002F2020\u002F03\u002F23\u002FMicrobiome-maintenance-Good-gut-health-could-tackle-COVID-19\"\u003Ehelp to stop you getting Covid-19\u003C\u002Fa\u003E (and it probably can&rsquo;t).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELike probiotics, kombucha contains live microorganisms. However, no studies have ever confirmed whether the drink has these in high enough concentrations to be considered one &ndash; and there is currently no evidence that kombucha specifically can treat or prevent any illnesses whatsoever.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe picture is less clear for probiotics in general.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-14"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"There is currently no evidence that any kind of probiotic can protect you from Covid-19","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200408-covid-19-can-boosting-your-immune-system-protect-you-15"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EOne 2015 review found that probiotics &ndash; beneficial microorganisms which are concentrated in foods, drinks, or pills &ndash; significantly \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.cochranelibrary.com\u002Fcdsr\u002Fdoi\u002F10.1002\u002F14651858.CD006895.pub3\u002Fabstract\"\u003Ereduced the number of&nbsp; upper respiratory tract infections\u003C\u002Fa\u003E that people got and made them less severe. They also slightly reduced the use of antibiotics and led to fewer school absences. The authors concluded that they might be better than placebo treatments, but pointed out that the quality of the available evidence was low.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E(You can find out more about \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20190121-what-we-do-and-dont-know-about-gut-health\"\u003Ewhat we do and don&rsquo;t know about gut health\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, as well as \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20190205-how-to-eat-your-way-to-a-healthy-gut\"\u003Ehow to eat your way to a healthy gut\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by checking out BBC Future&rsquo;s series on gut bacteria from last year. They shouldn't feel empowered to go out there and, you know, start having parties,&rdquo; she says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWellness products aside, there are some approaches you \u003Cem\u003Ecan \u003C\u002Fem\u003Etake to help support your immune system. They aren&rsquo;t especially sexy, and you won&rsquo;t see many wellness influencers selling them in a bottle. They are, however, proven to work &ndash; and they don&rsquo;t require shelling out your hard-earned cash: get enough sleep, exercise, eat a balanced diet, and try not to be stressed. &nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFailing that, there is one sure-fire way to improve your immunity to certain pathogens: vaccination.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E--\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EJoin one million Future fans by liking us on&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.facebook.com\u002FBBCFuture\u002F\"\u003EFacebook\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cem\u003E, or follow us on&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002FBBC_Future\"\u003ETwitter\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cem\u003E&nbsp;or\u003Cstrong\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.instagram.com\u002Fbbcfuture_official\u002F\"\u003EInstagram\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cem\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EIf you liked this story,&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fpages.emails.bbc.com\u002Fsubscribe\u002F?ocid=fut.bbc.email.we.email-signup\"\u003Esign up for the weekly bbc.com features newsletter\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cem\u003E, called &ldquo;The Essential List&rdquo;. There is an obvious historical example to be drawn upon in countries where epidemics of Mers and Sars, both caused by other types of coronavirus, have occurred previously. But even in Canada, which was a Sars hotspot, the impact of that knowledge seems to have melted away, says Jacalyn Duffin, professor emerita of the history of medicine at Queens University in Canada. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESars killed 44 people in the country in 2003, but that hasn&rsquo;t translated into consistent long-term planning to cope with future outbreaks of respiratory disease. &ldquo;Pandemic planning was really big in the immediate aftermath of Sars,&rdquo; says Duffin. &ldquo;It went on for a few years in Canada, but it&rsquo;s amazing how quickly it faded.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"We haven’t experienced this before, but we have experienced things that have similarities in some way – Robert West","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EEven for those who have lived through Mers or Sars, the Covid-19 pandemic is on another scale, Duffin says. And for people who haven&rsquo;t, when we turn to our own personal histories for comparison we draw a blank. &ldquo;We haven&rsquo;t experienced this before, but we have experienced things that have similarities in some way,&rdquo; says Robert West, a professor of behavioural science and health at University College London. &ldquo;Always what humans do when we&rsquo;re faced with a new situation is look for parallels.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn Europe, perhaps the first comparison that people have been reaching for, particularly the older generations, is wartime. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll notice some people talking about the Blitz,&rdquo; says West. &nbsp;&ldquo;There are some features that are similar there and some that are different.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe similarity, says West, is the central idea of existential threat. &ldquo;Not only was there the risk that they would be killed in the short term through their house being blown up, but there was the threat of invasion, which would be catastrophic. One thing that this coronavirus pandemic shares with that is the communal sense of anxiety.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-4"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088h06y"],"imageAlignment":"centre","imageAltText":"People in India queue at a community kitchen a safe distance apart (Credit: Getty Images)","imageCaption":"People in India queue at a community kitchen a safe distance apart (Credit: Getty Images)","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-5"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EThis parallel is useful in understanding people&rsquo;s response to public health guidance &ndash; to follow it vigilantly or to engage in avoidant behaviour, nonchalantly disregarding the advice to go to one&rsquo;s usual crowded Sunday market. &ldquo;You get some people walking around who are feeling perfectly fine because they don&rsquo;t think anything bad will happen and they&rsquo;ll take it as it comes,&rdquo; says West. &ldquo;And there will be others with a gnawing feeling of anxiety and foreboding from when they wake up to the end of the day and every moment in between.&rdquo; Wherever you fall on that spectrum, a crisis will bring out your response more sharply than ever, says West.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESo, how do you engage with someone who remains complacent amid a pandemic?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELeslie Martin, a health psychologist at La Sierra University in California and co-author of the Oxford Handbook on Health Communication, Behaviour Change and Treatment Adherence, says that without a precedent to compare the crisis to, we have to be a bit more creative. Martin suggests trying to picture a world months or years in the future, when the pandemic has played out.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;How will we look back on our behaviour? Will we have regrets about how we acted?&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;Even though we have not lived through this, think about how we will reflect on it later. It could help us to make better choices.&rdquo;\u003Cstrong\u003E \u003Cbr \u002F\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ERules to live by\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAnother major challenge in following public health guidance on coronavirus, Martin says, is the invisible, intangible nature of the spread of the virus. &ldquo;Human beings in general are much more responsive when there is immediate evidence of the outcome,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But because there is such a long incubation period, and because many people are asymptomatic in the beginning, it&rsquo;s not until too late that people think, &lsquo;I ought to have isolated more.&rsquo;&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.washingtonpost.com\u002Fgraphics\u002F2020\u002Fworld\u002Fcorona-simulator\u002F\"\u003EVisual imagery, like the dots used in many simulations\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, explain transmission and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200324-covid-19-how-social-distancing-can-beat-coronavirus\"\u003Ethe effectiveness of social distancing\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. If people who had the virus were immediately noticeable, like those colour-changing dots, one can imagine uninfected people would be rather more vigilant in remaining the recommended distance. In the absence of a clear marker, says Martin, the threat seems far off and hypothetical.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-6"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088h0fq"],"imageAlignment":"centre","imageAltText":"Passengers in Singapore wait for a bus (Credit: Getty Images)","imageCaption":"If we could see who was carrying the virus, perhaps we would be more vigilant about maintaining good health practices (Credit: Getty Images)","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-7"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EA third reason is that so many of our decisions are made on the fly. The choice of what to do on a minute-by-minute basis leaves us with competing options: shall I have another cookie now, or go for a run? Shall I wash my hands after going outside, or should I not bother?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;When people are making that kind of decision in the moment &ndash; and it&rsquo;s always in the moment &ndash; the thought is, &lsquo;It will be OK this time&rsquo;,&rdquo; says West. A good example is quitting smoking, he says. The pattern is to think that the next cigarette won&rsquo;t kill you so you keep going, when all the while the accumulation of cigarettes \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpmc\u002Farticles\u002FPMC4998250\u002F\"\u003Egreatly increases your risk of early death\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. &ldquo;So one of the tendencies is for people to think, &lsquo;It&rsquo;s going to be OK this time&rsquo;. We need people to understand that it won&rsquo;t.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIndeed, the evidence shows that people tend to be very poor at assessing risk and chance. It&rsquo;s one of the reasons why \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20161121-how-to-beat-the-fear-of-flying\"\u003Epeople are more likely to be afraid of flying than driving\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, even though the risk of an accident or death is so much higher in a car, or \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.emerald.com\u002Finsight\u002Fcontent\u002Fdoi\u002F10.1108\u002FIJCHM-07-2018-0577\u002Ffull\u002Fhtml\"\u003Ewhy people gamble when the odds are stacked against them\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-8"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"People have to think about the risks, not in terms of a judgment on each occasion, but as a set of rules that they always adhere to – Robert West","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-9"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EThe way out of this faulty evaluation of risk is to stop relying on decisions in the moment. &ldquo;People have to think about the risks, not in terms of a judgment on each occasion, but as a set of rules that they always adhere to,&rdquo; says West. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a simple equation: the more barriers you put in the way of that virus getting from one person&rsquo;s lungs into another person&rsquo;s lungs, the fewer infections there will be.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGetting the message\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESo, given these challenges &ndash; how can you talk to people who are not taking public health guidance seriously in a way that will be effective?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFirst, cite the medical institutions and practitioners best placed to provide information on the epidemic, such as independent public health bodies who ground their advice in evidence. &ldquo;The messenger has to be seen as an expert,&rdquo; says West. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re much more likely to take seriously advice from people who we think know what they are talking about.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESecond, make the message positive. &ldquo;Since we are asking people to be isolated, and it&rsquo;s not seen as a positive thing for most people, what we can do is frame that isolation in terms of something positive,&rdquo; says Martin. &ldquo;Perhaps it&rsquo;s having more time for something you might not otherwise, like catching up on reading.&rdquo; \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor elderly people, who may be less time-poor to start with, focusing on the opportunity to do something creative and useful might work, Martin adds, such as getting the garden in order or doing an art project they&rsquo;ve always wanted to do but haven&rsquo;t.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-10"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"Think about your grandchild or your parent. Think about other people and ask, ‘Do I want to take any additional risk that might harm them? – Leslie Martin","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-covid-19-how-to-convince-others-social-distancing-works-11"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EThird, appeal to their loved ones &ndash; and make it personal. &ldquo;We&rsquo;d do things for others that we wouldn&rsquo;t necessarily do for ourselves,&rdquo; says Martin. &ldquo;The more we can make these messages personal to the individual, the better off we&rsquo;ll be. Think about your grandchild or your parent. Think about other people and ask, &lsquo;Do I want to take any additional risk that might harm them?&rsquo;.&rdquo; The more personal the message, the more likely it will resonate, says Martin.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis is where mass communication from public health bodies might be less effective than word of mouth. &ldquo;People can be leaders within their own family and social groups, to set a good example of positive peer pressure,&rdquo; says Martin. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s tough to be the first one but that&rsquo;s a really valuable gift we can give, to be that example.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECovid-19 is proving to be \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.co.uk\u002Fnews\u002Fuk-51991566\"\u003Ea mind-bending disease that has made our usual signals of affection the most dangerous thing\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to the people we love. But, regardless of whether someone is a hard-partying twentysomething or a tough-minded octogenarian, appealing to the people and relationships they really value could be the best strategy to protect them.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E--\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EAs an award-winning science site, BBC Future is committed to bringing you evidence-based analysis and myth-busting stories around the new coronavirus. It can act as a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Facademic.oup.com\u002Fajcn\u002Farticle\u002F98\u002F6\u002F1700S\u002F4577506#110563412\"\u003Esalve for a troubled mind\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and can make us feel \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpmc\u002Farticles\u002FPMC2737341\u002F\"\u003Ecloser to other people\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. It can even help us \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F22574769\"\u003Ecool down in hot weather\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBut while many people might seek out a cup of coffee, a mug of tea or a hot toddie to help them in these difficult times, one thing a hot drink won&rsquo;t do is protect you from Covid-19.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere have been many claims to the contrary circulating widely on social media and in private messaging apps &ndash; one of the many \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Fnews\u002Fworld-51735367\"\u003Efake pieces of health advice\u003C\u002Fa\u003E that are being shared. One claim that hot water would be enough to protect people from the virus even led the United Nations Children&rsquo;s Fund (Unicef) to issue a statement to say that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftwitter.com\u002FUNICEFCambodia\u002Fstatus\u002F1235513993466281984\"\u003Eit had not come from them\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;There is no evidence that hot drinks will protect against viral infections,&rdquo; says Ron Eccles, an expert in respiratory diseases at Cardiff University in the UK and former director of the Common Cold Centre.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYou might also like:\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Cbr \u002F\u003E&bull; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200317-covid-19-how-long-does-the-coronavirus-last-on-surfaces\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EHow long does coronavirus last on surfaces?\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr \u002F\u003E&bull; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200324-covid-19-how-social-distancing-can-beat-coronavirus\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EWhy social distancing might last for some time\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr \u002F\u003E&bull; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200323-coronavirus-will-hot-weather-kill-covid-19\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EWill hotter weather kill the coronavirus?\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EEccles has conducted research in the past on the apparent effects of drinking hot liquids when suffering from cold and flu. He found that while a hot drink might bring some relief from the symptoms of a cold, it was likely partly due to the effect it had on promoting secretions of saliva and mucus in the mouth and nose, which soothe inflammation. But he also concluded that there was likely to be a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F19145994\"\u003Estrong placebo effect involved too\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"infographic","imageAlignment":"centre","imageOrientation":"landscape","pullQuoteImageAlignment":"centre","videoImageAlign":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19-0"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088ls4d"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19-1"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EThe hot drink does not, however, get rid of the virus causing the infection in the first place.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the case of Sars-CoV-2, the coronavirus that causes the disease Covid-19, BBC Future has already examined how drinking water doesn&rsquo;t \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200319-covid-19-will-drinking-water-keep-you-safe-from-coronavirus\"\u003Eoffer any protection against the new coronavirus\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. The virus cannot simply be washed away by drinking water or gargling liquid regularly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile it can enter the body through the nose and mouth in tiny droplets coughed out by other people who are infected, it primarily tends to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Flink.springer.com\u002Farticle\u002F10.1007\u002Fs00134-020-05985-9\"\u003Einfect cells of the respiratory tract\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. These cells carry an enzyme on their surface that the virus needs in order to get inside them.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EInhaling these droplets will transport them down towards the lungs &ndash; away from anywhere that can be reached by a mouthful of liquid.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"Guzzling down hot liquid is unlikely to raise the temperature enough in your respiratory tract to kill any of the virus inside the cells there","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EOnce inside the body, the virus also quickly gets inside the cells of its human host \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.medrxiv.org\u002Fcontent\u002F10.1101\u002F2020.03.26.20044487v1.full.pdf\"\u003Ewhere it then slowly replicates itself\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, meaning it is also able to comfortably hide from any attempts to wash it away. Some initial studies have suggested it \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.medrxiv.org\u002Fcontent\u002F10.1101\u002F2020.03.26.20044487v1.full.pdf\"\u003Ecan take up to 30 hours\u003C\u002Fa\u003E from the first cells being infected, to the point where the virus bursts out to infect more cells.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESimilarly, once inside our cells, the virus is protected from any extremes of temperature. The human body tends to sit at a comfortable 37C (98.6F), which is perfect for the virus to replicate and spread. Guzzling down hot liquid is unlikely to raise the temperature enough in your respiratory tract to kill any of the virus inside the cells there. It takes temperatures of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.who.int\u002Fcsr\u002Fsars\u002Fsurvival_2003_05_04\u002Fen\u002F\"\u003E56C (132.8F) or higher to effectively kill the closely related coronavirus\u003C\u002Fa\u003E that causes Sars, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpmc\u002Farticles\u002FPMC7086689\u002F\"\u003Ealthough some tests \u003C\u002Fa\u003Ehave shown \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.sciencedirect.com\u002Fscience\u002Farticle\u002Fpii\u002FS016609340400179X?via%3Dihub\"\u003Etemperatures higher than 60-65C (140-149F) are needed\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. While no studies have yet been published on the virus that causes Covid-19 and its resistance to high temperatures, it is likely that it will be similar to other coronaviruses.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile you can use temperatures of 70C (158F) and above to kill the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.who.int\u002Fdocs\u002Fdefault-source\u002Fcoronaviruse\u002Fsituation-reports\u002F20200221-sitrep-32-covid-19.pdf?sfvrsn=4802d089_2#page=2\"\u003Evirus on food by cooking it\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, it is \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.hse.gov.uk\u002Fhealthservices\u002Fscalding-burning.htm\"\u003Ehot enough to scald human skin\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and cause injuries. (\u003Cem\u003ERead more about \u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200317-covid-19-how-long-does-the-coronavirus-last-on-surfaces\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003Ehow long the coronavirus survives on surfaces and how to kill it\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cem\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19-4"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088ls8k"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200403-coronavirus-will-hot-drinks-protect-you-from-covid-19-5"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EIt&rsquo;s easy to see how climbing into a bath that hot, as some fake advice has suggested, would be hard to stand for long, and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.who.int\u002Femergencies\u002Fdiseases\u002Fnovel-coronavirus-2019\u002Fadvice-for-public\u002Fmyth-busters\"\u003Elikely do more harm than good\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Even if you could stay in water hot enough, it isn&rsquo;t going to kill any of the virus already inside your body. This is because, regardless of how hot you are on the outside, your body works hard to keep its temperature inside at around 37C (98.6F). You are more likely to burn yourself and do serious harm than kill the virus.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERaising your core body temperature even to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.archivesofpathology.org\u002Fdoi\u002Fpdf\u002F10.1043\u002F1543-2165%282006%29130%5B1297%3AHAHMIO%5D2.0.CO%3B2\"\u003E40C (104F) can lead to severe heat stroke\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Body temperatures higher than this \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.sciencedirect.com\u002Fscience\u002Farticle\u002Fpii\u002FS1566070216300017\"\u003Ecan lead to death\u003C\u002Fa\u003E unless treated quickly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESome misleading claims also state that compounds in tea can have a protective effect against the Covid-19 virus, but \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ffactcheck.afp.com\u002Fno-evidence-drinking-tea-can-cure-or-relieve-symptoms-covid-19-doctors-say\"\u003Ethere is no scientific evidence to support this\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, as the BBC has \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.co.uk\u002Fnews\u002Fblogs-trending-51931394\"\u003Ereported elsewhere\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESo that hot drink may be soothing. But as ever, the best way to protect yourself from the Covid-19 virus is to abide by social distancing measures, wash your hands with soap and water after touching potentially infected surfaces and follow the most up-to-date \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.who.int\u002Femergencies\u002Fdiseases\u002Fnovel-coronavirus-2019\u002Fadvice-for-public\"\u003Eofficial medical advice\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E--\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EAs an award-winning science site, BBC Future is committed to bringing you evidence-based analysis and myth-busting stories around the new coronavirus. Meanwhile in neighbouring Germany, the same virus led to fatality rates of just 1%. In China, it was 4%, while Israel had the lowest rate worldwide, at 0.35%.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAt first it can seem surprising that the same virus &ndash; which doesn&rsquo;t seem to have mutated significantly as it has spread &ndash; can lead to such widely differing reported mortality rates. And even within one country, the rate appears to change over time. So what&rsquo;s going on?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESeveral main factors account for much of the difference we&rsquo;re seeing &ndash; and perhaps the most important come down to simply how we&rsquo;re counting, as well as testing, cases.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EDiffering death rates\u003Cbr \u002F\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFirst, there is confusion about what people mean by &ldquo;death rate&rdquo;. This confusion can make countries&rsquo; numbers look vastly different, even if their populations are dying at the same rate.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere are, in fact, two kinds of fatality rate. The first is the proportion of people who die who have tested positive for the disease. This is called the &ldquo;case fatality rate&rdquo;. The second kind is the proportion of people who die after having the infection overall; as many of these will never be picked up, this figure has to be an estimate. This is the &ldquo;infection fatality rate&rdquo;.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn other words, the case fatality rate describes how many people doctors can be \u003Cem\u003Esure \u003C\u002Fem\u003Eare killed by the infection, versus how many people the virus kills overall, says Carl Heneghan, an epidemiologist and director of the Centre for Evidence-Based Medicine at the University of Oxford; he is also a GP in recovery from a suspected Covid-19 infection.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EYou might also like:\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fbbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200325-covid-19-the-history-of-pandemics\"\u003EWhy infectious diseases are on the rise\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200221-coronavirus-the-harmful-hunt-for-covid-19s-patient-zero\"\u003EThe hunt for Covid-19&rsquo;s &lsquo;patient zero&rsquo;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Ffuture\u002Farticle\u002F20200330-coronavirus-are-children-immune-to-covid-19\"\u003EWhy children are not immune to Covid-19\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETo see what a difference this makes, consider 100 people who have been infected with Covid-19. Ten of them have it so severely that they go into hospital, where they test positive for Covid-19. The other 90 are not tested at all. One of the hospital patients then dies from the virus. The other 99 people survive.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThat would give a case fatality rate of one in 10, or 10%. But the infection fatality rate would be just one in 100, or 1%.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"infographic","imageAlignment":"centre","imageOrientation":"landscape","pullQuoteImageAlignment":"centre","videoImageAlign":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-0"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"The lack of widespread, systematic testing in most countries is the main source of discrepancies in death rates internationally","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-1"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003ESo if some countries only test patients ill enough to go to hospital &ndash; and don&rsquo;t test the less-ill (or even asymptomatic) Covid-19 patients who don&rsquo;t get to hospital (which is what the UK is currently doing) &ndash; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.co.uk\u002Fnews\u002Fhealth-51943612\"\u003Ethe death rate can appear higher than in countries where testing is widespread\u003C\u002Fa\u003E (such as Germany or South Korea).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EImpact of testing\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EEven if you&rsquo;re careful to compare the same type of fatality rate across countries, it&rsquo;s easy to see how testing more, or fewer, people would change the results.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn fact, the lack of widespread, systematic testing in most countries is the main source of discrepancies in death rates internationally, says Dietrich Rothenbacher, director of the Institute of Epidemiology and Medical&nbsp;Biometry at the University of Ulm in Germany.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs a result, the current figures are &ldquo;not at all&rdquo; directly comparable between countries, he says. This is because, to get an accurate figure across a population, it is necessary to test not just symptomatic cases, but asymptomatic people too. Having that data would give an accurate picture of how the pandemic is affecting whole populations, not just the sick.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Currently we have a huge bias in the numbers coming from different countries &ndash; therefore the data are not directly comparable,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;What we need to really have valid and comparable numbers would be a defined and systematic way to choose a representative sampling frame.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.theguardian.com\u002Fcommentisfree\u002F2020\u002Fmar\u002F20\u002Feradicated-coronavirus-mass-testing-covid-19-italy-vo\"\u003Evillage of V&ograve; in northern Italy is an example of why testing is important not just to get accurate data, but to contain Covid-19\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. When the first Covid-19 case in V&ograve; was confirmed, testing was rolled out to the entire village of 3,300 people. The results showed that at the time of the &ldquo;first case&rdquo;, 3% of the village was already infected, but showing no or few symptoms.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088fv0k"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EA widespread testing programme in Iceland shows a similar picture. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fnordiclifescience.org\u002Fcovid-19-first-results-of-the-voluntary-screening-in-iceland\u002F\"\u003EIceland has tested more than 3% of its population of around 365,000 people so far\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, both those who show symptoms and those who don&rsquo;t. By extrapolating the results, the testing programme estimated that 0.5% of Iceland&rsquo;s population was likely to have had Covid-19. But even this figure may be slightly low because asymptomatic people are less likely to seek out testing, notes Heneghan. It is thought that the actual figure could be closer to 1% of Iceland&rsquo;s population, which would mean roughly 3,650 infections.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAn added difficulty is that this data is not from peer-reviewed research, but rather is almost real-time clinical data &ndash; which can be messy and come with many caveats. What figures like these do underline, says Sheila Bird of the University of Cambridge&rsquo;s MRC Biostatistics Unit, is the importance of widespread testing to help inform public health measures.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;If you never actually develop symptoms but had encountered the virus, that would be an infection, but it is an &lsquo;uncountable&rsquo; one &ndash; uncountable until the time that we have an antibody test,&rdquo; says Bird.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAntibody tests detect the traces of an immune response to the virus and reveal who has had an infection. Those tests are the game-changers that could reveal who has developed immunity to the virus and can safely return to daily life without risk of infection or of spreading the virus. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s why the development of that test and its deployment is so terribly important,&rdquo; says Bird.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn V&ograve;, the spread of Covid-19 was halted after two weeks, as both the widespread testing and strict follow-up measures allowed for the targeted and effective containment of infections. Iceland, so far, has had only two Covid-19 deaths.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhat counts as a Covid-19 death? \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere are other factors that alter the death rate, too.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne of them is what doctors actually count as a Covid-19 death. At first it might seem simple enough: if a patient dies while infected with Covid-19, they died of Covid-19.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-4"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088cpw7"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-5"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EBut what if they had an underlying condition, such as asthma, which was exacerbated by Covid-19? Or what if the patient died from something seemingly less related to Covid-19, which is a respiratory disease &ndash; such as, say, a brain aneurysm? Which condition should be considered the cause of death?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.co.uk\u002Fnews\u002Fhealth-52103808\"\u003EEven within a country, official statistics can vary according to what you count\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. In the UK, for example, the Department of Health and Social Care releases daily updates on how many people who tested positive for Covid-19 died that day. This includes any patient who tested positive for Covid-19 but who might have died from another condition (for example, terminal cancer). But the UK&rsquo;s Office for National Statistics counts all deaths as Covid-19 where Covid-19 was mentioned on the death certificate, regardless of whether they were tested or if it was merely a suspected case of Covid-19. Adding to the complexity of trying to understand the death rates is that the two are out of sync, since the ONS way of counting can only happen after a death certificate has been issued, so takes longer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;The issue is not really about right or wrong, but about each source of data having its own strengths and weaknesses,&rdquo; Sarah Caul, head of mortality analysis at ONS, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.ons.gov.uk\u002F2020\u002F03\u002F31\u002Fcounting-deaths-involving-the-coronavirus-covid-19\u002F\"\u003Ewrites in a blog post on the different ways of counting deaths\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis is not necessarily a source of discrepancy \u003Cem\u003Ebetween\u003C\u002Fem\u003E most countries, though, as many are counting deaths in the same way. Italy counts any death of a patient who has Covid-19 as a death caused by Covid-19; so does Germany and Hong Kong.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the US, doctors have more discretion: they are asked to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.cdc.gov\u002Fcoronavirus\u002F2019-ncov\u002Fdownloads\u002FCOVID-19-Persons-Under-Investigation-and-Case-Report-Form-Instructions.pdf\"\u003Erecord whether the patient died &ldquo;as a result of this illness&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E when reporting Covid-19 deaths to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. It could be easy to see how a physician might believe that a Covid-19 patient who died of, say, a heart attack or brain aneurysm didn&rsquo;t die as a \u003Cem\u003Eresult \u003C\u002Fem\u003Eof Covid-19, and so wouldn&rsquo;t report accordingly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EImportantly, though, while this might make a difference when the data is analysed months or years from now, this doesn&rsquo;t translate into any difference in the death statistics at the moment. At present in the US, any death of a Covid-19 patient, no matter what the physician believes to be the direct cause, is counted for public reporting as a Covid-19 death.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;I expect that the final death certificate will have Covid along with pre-existing conditions, should there be any,&rdquo; says C&eacute;cile Viboud, an epidemiologist at the Fogarty International Center at the National Institutes of Health. &ldquo;But at this point, any Covid-positive case who dies will be tallied in the US death count.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESo what counts as a Covid-19 death will affect our overall understanding of the lethality of the disease in the long run, but it most likely isn&rsquo;t playing a huge factor between countries.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EComplex causes\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe picture is murkier still when patients have not had a Covid-19 test, but are a suspected case. Given that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.cdc.gov\u002Fcoronavirus\u002F2019-ncov\u002Fneed-extra-precautions\u002Fpeople-at-higher-risk.html?CDC_AA_refVal=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cdc.gov%2Fcoronavirus%2F2019-ncov%2Fspecific-groups%2Fpeople-at-higher-risk.html\"\u003Emany deaths from Covid-19 are in people who have underlying health issues\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, doctors still have to make the call on the cause of death.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-6"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088crmy"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-7"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EDuring an epidemic, doctors are more likely to attribute a death with complex causes as being caused by the disease in question &ndash; a trait known as \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fcatalogofbias.org\u002Fbiases\u002Fascertainment-bias\u002F\"\u003Eascertainment bias\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;We know, during an epidemic, people will call every death as though it&rsquo;s related to Covid-19. But that is not the case,&rdquo; says Heneghan. &ldquo;Always, when people look back at the case notes and assign causation, they realise they will have overestimated the case fatality in relation to the disease.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe reason for the bias is that &ldquo;there&rsquo;s a tendency to focus on the worst-case scenario&rdquo;, says Heneghan. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s the only message that gets out there.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne example is the H1N1 pandemic of 2009, known as swine flu. Early case fatality rate estimates were inflated by a factor of more than 10. Even 10 weeks into the epidemic, estimates \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.eurosurveillance.org\u002Fcontent\u002F10.2807\u002Fese.14.33.19309-en\"\u003Evaried widely between countries, coming in between 0.1% and 5.1%\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. When medics later had a chance to go through case documents and evaluate cases, the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.reuters.com\u002Farticle\u002Fus-health-flu-who\u002Fworld-must-prepare-for-inevitable-next-flu-pandemic-who-says-idUSKBN1QS1EP\"\u003Eactual H1N1 case death rate was far lower, at 0.02%\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThat&rsquo;s not a cause for complacency, says Heneghan. But it could be an antidote to some of the alarm at the very high reported death rates in some countries.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EHidden deaths\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile over-counting deaths among recorded Covid-19 cases may lead to overestimation of the death rate, there is another factor that could mean the death rate is also &ndash; confusingly &ndash; being \u003Cem\u003Eunderestimated\u003C\u002Fem\u003E at the same time.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis is the problem of hidden deaths from Covid-19: those people who die from the disease who are never tested. This comes into play when health services are overwhelmed and even those patients who have severe symptoms of the virus are not taken into hospital to be tested and treated, simply because there isn&rsquo;t capacity.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the small Italian town of Nembro, in Lombardy, only 31 people have officially died from Covid-19. But one preliminary study has found that it&rsquo;s likely that far more people have died from Covid-19. That&rsquo;s because the overall death rate &ndash; not just from Covid-19, but from all causes &ndash; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.corriere.it\u002Fpolitica\u002F20_marzo_26\u002Fthe-real-death-toll-for-covid-19-is-at-least-4-times-the-official-numbers-b5af0edc-6eeb-11ea-925b-a0c3cdbe1130.shtml\"\u003Ewas four times higher this year than in the same period last year\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Ordinarily, around 35 people die in the first months of the year in Nembro. This year, 158 people were registered to have died.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis jump in excess deaths is speculated to be down to undiagnosed and untested cases of Covid-19.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-8"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"There’s nothing to say that going into an intensive care unit is going to have a better outcome compared with care in the community - Carl Heneghan","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-9"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EThe number of hospital beds available could also play a role, as countries with lower capacity in their health services may have to start making decisions sooner about which Covid-19 cases to prioritise for treatment. This could lead to more Covid-19 deaths in the community that go untested (and uncounted), as people with symptoms stay away.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile this might lead to more \u003Cem\u003Euncounted\u003C\u002Fem\u003E deaths, it may not necessarily translate into to more deaths overall. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s nothing to say that going into an intensive care unit is going to have a better outcome compared with care in the community,&rdquo; says Heneghan.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMore important than the number of beds available in hospitals, he says, is the way the beds are organised. If Covid-19 patients are in close proximity to other patients &ndash; or if doctors move between Covid-19 and non-Covid-19 wards &ndash; it heightens the risk of spreading the disease.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;That is why you need separate hospitals for separate infection teams,&rdquo; he says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWhat role does age play?\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs well as differences caused by clinical definitions of what counts as a Covid-19 death and the numbers of people going untested, there are other factors that mean the virus hits some countries harder than others.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne that has been put forward by Italian doctors is the age profile of a country. In 2019, nearly a quarter of the Italian population was 65 years or older, compared to only 11% in China. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fjamanetwork.com\u002Fjournals\u002Fjama\u002Ffullarticle\u002F2763667\"\u003EThe overall case fatality rate in Italy as of mid-March was 7.2% &ndash; much higher than China&rsquo;s rate of 2.3% at a comparable stage of its epidemic\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. But from the ages of zero to 69, the two country&rsquo;s case fatality rates are comparable, note researchers from the Istituto Superiore di Sanit&agrave; in Rome.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-10"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088cq4s"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-11"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EAmong the oldest patients, though, Italy and China part ways. The 70-79 age group in Italy had a case fatality rate of 12.8%, while China&rsquo;s was 8%. For the over-80s, the difference was more pronounced still: Italy&rsquo;s was 20.2% and China&rsquo;s 14.8%. The reason for this discrepancy is still a bit of a mystery, the researchers note.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHeneghan suspects that one factor at play in Italy&rsquo;s high figures could be not to do with the virus itself, but with bacteria. The country has the highest numbers of deaths due to antimicrobial resistance in the EU &ndash; in fact, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ansa.it\u002Fenglish\u002Fnews\u002Fscience_tecnology\u002F2019\u002F11\u002F19\u002Fitaly-top-in-eu-in-antibiotic-resistance_369e0123-0107-445e-8c17-f11932c9d27c.html\"\u003Ea third of all EU deaths from antimicrobial resistance happen in\u003C\u002Fa\u003E Italy. While antibiotics do precisely nothing to tackle a virus, a viral infection can often open the way for secondary infections or complications like bacterial pneumonia. If that then can&rsquo;t be treated properly with antibiotics because the bacteria is resistant, then this can be what kills the patient, not the virus itself.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;This is an incredibly important part of the whole story,&rdquo; says Heneghan. &ldquo;And it is particularly prevalent in the elderly.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs well as age, the overall health of the population has been highlighted as another contributing factor &ndash; particularly given that people who have underlying health problems are more vulnerable. While that might play a contributing role, this doesn&rsquo;t explain why some countries are reporting more Covid-19 deaths than others: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bloomberg.com\u002Fnews\u002Farticles\u002F2019-02-24\u002Fspain-tops-italy-as-world-s-healthiest-nation-while-u-s-slips\"\u003EItaly is consistently ranked as one of the world&rsquo;s healthiest countries\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, for example, and has \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fgamapserver.who.int\u002FmapLibrary\u002FFiles\u002FMaps\u002FGlobal_HALE_2016.png\"\u003Ea longer healthy life expectancy than China\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-12"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088fvjn"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"future\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-coronavirus-why-death-and-mortality-rates-differ-13"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003ETrying to get a handle on exactly how lethal Covid-19 is will prove a knotty problem for some time yet. It may never be possible to get a true, reliable count of the deaths due to Covid-19, as testing in many parts of the world has been too slow to roll out.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs time goes on, the estimate of the Covid-19 death rate is likely to improve, as clinicians are eventually able to go through case notes and tease out the tangle of factors that contributed to each Covid-19 patient&rsquo;s death.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor now, while hospitals are filled with acutely ill patients and doctors and nurses are working overtime to care for them, that careful analysis is going to have to wait.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E--\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EAs an award-winning science site, BBC Future is committed to bringing you evidence-based analysis and myth-busting stories around the new coronavirus.
